     <p>
      <b>
       Sustainable development
      </b>
      is a process for meeting
      <a href="/wiki/Human_development_(humanity)" title="Human development (humanity)">
       human development
      </a>
      goals while
      <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">
       sustaining
      </a>
      the ability of natural systems to continue to provide the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_resources" title="Natural resources">
       natural resources
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">
       ecosystem services
      </a>
      upon which the
      <a href="/wiki/Economy" title="Economy">
       economy
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">
       society
      </a>
      depends. While the modern concept of sustainable development is derived most strongly from the 1987
      <a href="/wiki/Brundtland_Commission" title="Brundtland Commission">
       Brundtland Report
      </a>
      , it is rooted in earlier ideas about
      <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_forest_management" title="Sustainable forest management">
       sustainable forest management
      </a>
      and twentieth century environmental concerns. As the concept developed, it has shifted to focus more on
      <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">
       economic development
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Social_change" title="Social change">
       social development
      </a>
      and environmental protection for future generations.
     </p>
     <p>
      Sustainable development is the
      <a href="/wiki/Organizing_principle" title="Organizing principle">
       organizing principle
      </a>
      for sustaining finite resources necessary to provide for the needs of future generations of life on the planet. It is a process that envisions a desirable future state for human societies in which living conditions and resource-use continue to meet human needs without undermining the "integrity, stability and beauty" of natural
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biotic_community" title="Biotic community">
       biotic systems
      </a>
      . It was suggested that "the term 'sustainability' should be viewed as humanity's target goal of human-ecosystem equilibrium (homeostasis), while 'sustainable development' refers to the holistic approach and temporal processes that lead us to the end point of sustainability." (305)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3002" data-file-width="3000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/220px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/330px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg/440px-The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Earth_seen_from_Apollo_17.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Blue_Marble" title="The Blue Marble">
         The Blue Marble
        </a>
        , photographed from
        <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">
         Apollo 17
        </a>
        in 1972, quickly became an icon of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_conservation" title="Environmental conservation">
         environmental conservation
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-2">
          [2]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Sustainability can be defined as the practice of maintaining processes of productivity indefinitely—natural or human made—by replacing resources used with resources of equal or greater value without degrading or endangering natural biotic systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sustainable development ties together concern for the
      <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">
       carrying capacity
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Systems_ecology" title="Systems ecology">
       natural systems
      </a>
      with the social, political, and economic challenges faced by humanity.
      <a href="/wiki/Sustainability_science" title="Sustainability science">
       Sustainability science
      </a>
      is the study of the concepts of sustainable development and environmental science. There is an additional focus on the present generations' responsibility to regenerate, maintain and improve planetary resources for use by future generations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finn2_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Finn2-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Sustainable development has its roots in ideas about
      <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_forest_management" title="Sustainable forest management">
       sustainable forest management
      </a>
      which were developed in Europe during the seventeenth and eighteenth centuries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grober2_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Grober2-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blewitt.2C_p.6-162_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Blewitt.2C_p.6-162-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In response to a growing awareness of the depletion of timber resources in England,
      <a href="/wiki/John_Evelyn" title="John Evelyn">
       John Evelyn
      </a>
      argued that "sowing and planting of trees had to be regarded as a national duty of every landowner, in order to stop the destructive over-exploitation of natural resources" in his 1662 essay
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sylva,_or_A_Discourse_of_Forest-Trees_and_the_Propagation_of_Timber" title="Sylva, or A Discourse of Forest-Trees and the Propagation of Timber">
        Sylva
       </a>
      </i>
      . In 1713
      <a href="/wiki/Hans_Carl_von_Carlowitz" title="Hans Carl von Carlowitz">
       Hans Carl von Carlowitz
      </a>
      , a senior mining administrator in the service of Elector
      <a href="/wiki/Augustus_II_the_Strong" title="Augustus II the Strong">
       Frederick Augustus I of Saxony
      </a>
      published
      <i>
       Sylvicultura oeconomica
      </i>
      , a 400-page work on forestry. Building upon the ideas of Evelyn and French minister
      <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Colbert" title="Jean-Baptiste Colbert">
       Jean-Baptiste Colbert
      </a>
      , von Carlowitz developed the concept of managing forests for
      <a href="/wiki/Sustained_yield" title="Sustained yield">
       sustained yield
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grober2_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Grober2-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      His work influenced others, including
      <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt" title="Alexander von Humboldt">
       Alexander von Humboldt
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Georg_Ludwig_Hartig" title="Georg Ludwig Hartig">
       Georg Ludwig Hartig
      </a>
      , eventually leading to the development of a science of forestry. This in turn influenced people like
      <a href="/wiki/Gifford_Pinchot" title="Gifford Pinchot">
       Gifford Pinchot
      </a>
      , first head of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Forest_Service" title="US Forest Service">
       US Forest Service
      </a>
      , whose approach to forest management was driven by the idea of wise use of resources, and
      <a href="/wiki/Aldo_Leopold" title="Aldo Leopold">
       Aldo Leopold
      </a>
      whose
      <a href="/wiki/Land_ethic" title="Land ethic">
       land ethic
      </a>
      was influential in the development of the
      <a href="/wiki/Environmental_movement" title="Environmental movement">
       environmental movement
      </a>
      in the 1960s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grober2_5-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Grober2-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blewitt.2C_p.6-162_6-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Blewitt.2C_p.6-162-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Following the publication of
      <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Carson" title="Rachel Carson">
       Rachel Carson
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Silent_Spring" title="Silent Spring">
        Silent Spring
       </a>
      </i>
      in 1962, the developing environmental movement drew attention to the relationship between economic growth and development and environmental degradation.
      <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_E._Boulding" title="Kenneth E. Boulding">
       Kenneth E. Boulding
      </a>
      in his influential 1966 essay
      <i>
       The Economics of the Coming Spaceship Earth
      </i>
      identified the need for the economic system to fit itself to the ecological system with its limited pools of resources.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blewitt.2C_p.6-162_6-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Blewitt.2C_p.6-162-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      One of the first uses of the term sustainable in the contemporary sense was by the
      <a href="/wiki/Club_of_Rome" title="Club of Rome">
       Club of Rome
      </a>
      in 1972 in its classic report on the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limits_to_Growth" title="Limits to Growth">
        Limits to Growth
       </a>
      </i>
      , written by a group of scientists led by
      <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Meadows" title="Dennis Meadows">
       Dennis
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Donella_Meadows" title="Donella Meadows">
       Donella Meadows
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">
       Massachusetts Institute of Technology
      </a>
      . Describing the desirable "state of global equilibrium", the authors wrote: "We are searching for a model output that represents a world system that is sustainable without sudden and uncontrolled collapse and capable of satisfying the basic material requirements of all of its people."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finn2_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Finn2-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1980 the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Union_for_the_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for the Conservation of Nature">
       International Union for the Conservation of Nature
      </a>
      published a world conservation strategy that included one of the first references to sustainable development as a global priority
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and introduced the term "sustainable development".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Two years later, the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">
       United Nations
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/World_Charter_for_Nature" title="World Charter for Nature">
       World Charter for Nature
      </a>
      raised five principles of
      <a href="/wiki/Conservation_(ethic)" title="Conservation (ethic)">
       conservation
      </a>
      by which human conduct affecting nature is to be guided and judged.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1987 the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">
       United Nations
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Commission_on_Environment_and_Development" title="World Commission on Environment and Development">
       World Commission on Environment and Development
      </a>
      released the report
      <i>
       Our Common Future
      </i>
      , commonly called the Brundtland Report. The report included what is now one of the most widely recognised definitions of sustainable development.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brundtland2_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brundtland2-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
      <tr>
       <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">
        “
       </td>
       <td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">
        Sustainable development is development that meets the needs of the present without compromising the ability of future generations to meet their own needs. It contains within it two key concepts:
        <br/>
        · The concept of 'needs', in particular, the essential needs of the world's poor, to which overriding priority should be given; and
        <br/>
        · The idea of limitations imposed by the state of technology and social organization on the environment's ability to meet present and future needs.
       </td>
       <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">
        ”
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td class="cquotecite" colspan="3" style="border: none; padding-right: 4%; font-size: smaller; text-align: right;">
        <cite>
         —
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Commission_on_Environment_and_Development" title="World Commission on Environment and Development">
          World Commission on Environment and Development
         </a>
         ,
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Our_Common_Future" title="Our Common Future">
           Our Common Future
          </a>
         </i>
         (1987)
        </cite>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Since the Brundtland Report, the concept of sustainable development has developed beyond the initial intergenerational framework to focus more on the goal of "socially inclusive and environmentally sustainable economic growth".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1992, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_Conference_on_Environment_and_Development" title="UN Conference on Environment and Development">
       UN Conference on Environment and Development
      </a>
      published the
      <a href="/wiki/Earth_Charter" title="Earth Charter">
       Earth Charter
      </a>
      , which outlines the building of a just, sustainable, and peaceful global society in the 21st century. The action plan
      <a href="/wiki/Agenda_21" title="Agenda 21">
       Agenda 21
      </a>
      for sustainable development identified information, integration, and participation as key building blocks to help countries achieve development that recognises these interdependent pillars. It emphasises that in sustainable development everyone is a user and provider of information. It stresses the need to change from old sector-centered ways of doing business to new approaches that involve cross-sectoral co-ordination and the integration of environmental and social concerns into all development processes. Furthermore, Agenda 21 emphasises that broad public participation in decision making is a fundamental prerequisite for achieving sustainable development.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Under the principles of the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Charter" title="United Nations Charter">
       United Nations Charter
      </a>
      the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Millennium_Declaration" title="United Nations Millennium Declaration">
       Millennium Declaration
      </a>
      identified principles and treaties on sustainable development, including
      <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">
       economic development
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Social_change" title="Social change">
       social development
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Environmental_protection" title="Environmental protection">
       environmental protection
      </a>
      . Broadly defined, sustainable development is a systems approach to growth and development and to manage natural, produced, and
      <a href="/wiki/Social_capital" title="Social capital">
       social capital
      </a>
      for the welfare of their own and future generations. The term sustainable development as used by the United Nations incorporates both issues associated with
      <a href="/wiki/Land_development" title="Land development">
       land development
      </a>
      and broader issues of
      <a href="/wiki/Human_development_(humanity)" title="Human development (humanity)">
       human development
      </a>
      such as education, public health, and standard of living.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A 2013 study concluded that sustainability reporting should be reframed through the lens of four interconnected domains: ecology, economics, politics and culture.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="The_Sustainable_Development_Goals_.28SDGs.29">
       The Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainable_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: The Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In September 2015, the United Nations General Assembly formally adopted the "universal, integrated and transformative"
      <a class="external text" href="https://sustainabledevelopment.un.org/sdgs" rel="nofollow">
       2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development
      </a>
      , a set of 17 Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs). The goals are to be implemented and achieved in every country from the year 2016 to 2030.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Dimensions">
       Dimensions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainable_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Dimensions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Planetary_boundaries" title="Planetary boundaries">
       Planetary boundaries
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Triple_bottom_line" title="Triple bottom line">
       Triple bottom line
      </a>
     </div>
     <table style="float:right; clear:right; border: 1px solid #BBB; margin-left: 1em; margin-top: 1.2em; align: right auto 1em; padding:0.1em; background-color: #f4f6f7;">
      <tr style="background-color: white;">
       <td style="border: 1px solid lightgray;">
        <div class="center">
         <div class="floatnone">
          <div class="noresize" style="height: 153px; width: 240px; ">
           <map name="ImageMap_1_1224837793">

            </area>
            <area alt="Equitable" coords="97,66,100,68,103,70,105,73,108,75,110,79,111,83,113,86,120,84,125,82,131,79,136,76,140,72,143,69,146,64,149,58,145,57,139,56,127,56,119,57,113,59,106,61" href="/wiki/Equity_(economics)" shape="poly" title="Equitable">

            </area>

            </area>
            <area alt="Economic" coords="150,58,156,59,162,62,168,65,175,68,181,74,185,80,187,86,188,92,187,98,185,103,182,108,179,112,174,116,169,119,161,123,155,125,148,127,140,128,130,128,124,127,119,126,113,125,106,123,101,120,97,118,101,116,105,112,108,108,112,99,113,94,113,90,113,87,116,86,123,84,137,77,144,70" href="/wiki/Economics" shape="poly" title="Economic">
            </area>
            <area alt="Social" coords="42,56,42,52,43,44,46,38,51,32,57,26,65,23,73,20,84,17,93,16,106,16,114,18,125,21,134,26,138,30,143,34,146,38,149,44,150,49,150,53,150,57,141,56,136,55,123,56,111,58,101,62,96,65,92,62,84,59,77,57,69,56,60,55,60,55,60,55" href="/wiki/Society" shape="poly" title="Social">
            </area>
           </map>
           <img alt="Sustainable development.svg" data-file-height="510" data-file-width="800" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Sustainable_development.svg/240px-Sustainable_development.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Sustainable_development.svg/360px-Sustainable_development.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Sustainable_development.svg/480px-Sustainable_development.svg.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_1_1224837793" width="240"/>
           <div style="margin-left: 220px; margin-top: -20px; text-align: left;">
            <a href="/wiki/File:Sustainable_development.svg" title="About this image">
             <img alt="About this image" src="/w/extensions/ImageMap/desc-20.png?15600" style="border: none;"/>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr style="font-size: 87%; line-height:134%">
       <td style="border: 0px;">
        Scheme of sustainable development:
        <br/>
        at the confluence of three constituent parts. (2006)
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Sustainable development, or sustainability, has been described in terms of three spheres, dimensions, domains or pillars, i.e. the environment, the economy and society. The three-sphere framework was initially proposed by the economist
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ren%C3%A9_Passet&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="René Passet (page does not exist)">
       René Passet
      </a>
      in 1979.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has also been worded as "economic, environmental and social" or "ecology, economy and equity."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Sustainability_Report_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Global_Sustainability_Report-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This has been expanded by some authors to include a fourth pillar of culture,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      institutions or governance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Sustainability_Report_16-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Global_Sustainability_Report-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental">
       Environmental
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainable_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Environmental">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_engineering" title="Ecological engineering">
       Ecological engineering
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">

       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Human_welfare_and_ecological_footprint_sustainability.jpg">
