     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Progress">
       Progress
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainable_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Progress">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sustainable_development_goals" title="Sustainable development goals">
       Sustainable development goals
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Conference_on_Sustainable_Development" title="United Nations Conference on Sustainable Development">
       United Nations Conference on Sustainable Development
      </a>
      (UNCSD; also known as Rio 2012) was the third international conference on sustainable development, which aimed at reconciling the economic and environmental goals of the global community. An outcome of this conference was the development of the
      <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">
       Sustainable Development Goals
      </a>
      that aim to promote sustainable progress and eliminate inequalities around the world. However, few nations met the
      <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">
       World Wide Fund for Nature
      </a>
      's definition of sustainable development criteria established in 2006.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although some nations are more developed than others, all nations are constantly developing because each nation struggles with perpetuating disparities, inequalities and unequal access to fundamental rights and freedoms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">
       Measurement
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainable_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Measurement">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_footprint" title="Ecological footprint">
       Ecological footprint
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sustainability_measurement" title="Sustainability measurement">
       Sustainability measurement
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amazonie_deforestation.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="618" data-file-width="778" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Amazonie_deforestation.jpg/250px-Amazonie_deforestation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Amazonie_deforestation.jpg/375px-Amazonie_deforestation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Amazonie_deforestation.jpg/500px-Amazonie_deforestation.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amazonie_deforestation.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Deforestation and increased road-building in the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_Rainforest" title="Amazon Rainforest">
         Amazon Rainforest
        </a>
        are a concern because of increased human encroachment upon
        <a href="/wiki/Wilderness" title="Wilderness">
         wilderness
        </a>
        areas, increased resource extraction and further threats to
        <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">
         biodiversity
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 2007 a report for the U.S. Environmental Protection Agency stated: "While much discussion and effort has gone into sustainability indicators, none of the resulting systems clearly tells us whether our society is sustainable. At best, they can tell us that we are heading in the wrong direction, or that our current activities are not sustainable. More often, they simply draw our attention to the existence of problems, doing little to tell us the origin of those problems and nothing to tell us how to solve them."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nevertheless, a majority of authors assume that a set of well defined and harmonised indicators is the only way to make sustainability tangible. Those indicators are expected to be identified and adjusted through empirical observations (trial and error).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The most common critiques are related to issues like data quality, comparability, objective function and the necessary resources.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However a more general criticism is coming from the project management community: How can a sustainable development be achieved at global level if we cannot monitor it in any single project?
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Cuban-born researcher and entrepreneur Sonia Bueno suggests an alternative approach that is based upon the integral, long-term cost-benefit relationship as a measure and monitoring tool for the sustainability of every project, activity or enterprise.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        [94]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Furthermore, this concept aims to be a practical guideline towards sustainable development following the principle of conservation and increment of value rather than restricting the consumption of resources.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Reasonable qualifications of sustainability are seen U.S. Green Building Council's (USGBC) Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design (LEED). This design incorporates some ecological, economic, and social elements. The goals presented by LEED design goals are sustainable sites, water efficiency, energy and atmospheric emission reduction, material and resources efficiency, and indoor environmental quality. Although amount of structures for sustainability development is many, these qualification has become a standard for sustainable building.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Recent research efforts created also the
      <a href="/wiki/SDEWES_Centre#SDEWES_Index" title="SDEWES Centre">
       SDEWES Index
      </a>
      to benchmark the performance of cities across aspects that are related to energy, water and environment systems. The
      <a href="/wiki/SDEWES_Centre" title="SDEWES Centre">
       SDEWES
      </a>
      Index consists of 7 dimensions, 35 indicators, and close to 20 sub-indicators. It is currently applied to 58 cities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_capital">
       Natural capital
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainable_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Natural capital">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg">
        <img alt="Deforastation of native rain forest in Rio de Janeiro City for extraction of clay for civil construction" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1696" data-file-width="2544" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg/300px-Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg/450px-Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg/600px-Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">
         Deforestation
        </a>
        of native
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rain_forest" title="Rain forest">
         rain forest
        </a>
        in Rio de Janeiro City for extraction of
        <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">
         clay
        </a>
        for
        <a href="/wiki/Civil_engineering" title="Civil engineering">
         civil engineering
        </a>
        (2009 picture).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The sustainable development debate is based on the assumption that societies need to manage three types of capital (economic, social, and natural), which may be non-substitutable and whose consumption might be irreversible.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Leading
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_economics" title="Ecological economics">
       ecological economist
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Steady-state_economy#Herman_Daly.27s_concept_of_a_steady-state_economy" title="Steady-state economy">
       steady-state theorist
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Herman_Daly" title="Herman Daly">
       Herman Daly
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hd01_38-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-hd01-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      for example, points to the fact that
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_capital" title="Natural capital">
       natural capital
      </a>
      can not necessarily be substituted by economic capital. While it is possible that we can find ways to replace some natural resources, it is much more unlikely that they will ever be able to replace eco-system services, such as the protection provided by the ozone layer, or the climate stabilizing function of the Amazonian forest. In fact natural capital, social capital and economic capital are often complementarities. A further obstacle to substitutability lies also in the multi-functionality of many natural resources. Forests, for example, not only provide the raw material for paper (which can be substituted quite easily), but they also maintain biodiversity, regulate water flow, and absorb CO2.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another problem of natural and social capital deterioration lies in their partial irreversibility. The loss of biodiversity, for example, is often definitive. The same can be true for cultural diversity. For example, with globalisation advancing quickly the number of indigenous languages is dropping at alarming rates. Moreover, the depletion of natural and social capital may have non-linear consequences. Consumption of natural and social capital may have no observable impact until a certain threshold is reached. A lake can, for example, absorb nutrients for a long time while actually increasing its productivity. However, once a certain level of algae is reached lack of oxygen causes the lake's ecosystem to break down suddenly.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Business-as-usual">
       Business-as-usual
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainable_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Business-as-usual">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Air_.pollution_1.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="750" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Air_.pollution_1.jpg/240px-Air_.pollution_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Air_.pollution_1.jpg/360px-Air_.pollution_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Air_.pollution_1.jpg/480px-Air_.pollution_1.jpg 2x" width="240"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Air_.pollution_1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Before
        <a href="/wiki/Flue-gas_desulfurization" title="Flue-gas desulfurization">
         flue-gas desulfurization
        </a>
        was installed, the
        <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">
         air-polluting
        </a>
        emissions from this power plant in
        <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">
         New Mexico
        </a>
        contained excessive amounts of
        <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">
         sulfur dioxide
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      If the degradation of natural and social capital has such important consequence the question arises why action is not taken more systematically to alleviate it. Cohen and Winn
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
      point to four types of market failure as possible explanations: First, while the benefits of natural or social capital depletion can usually be privatised, the costs are often externalised (i.e. they are borne not by the party responsible but by society in general). Second, natural capital is often undervalued by society since we are not fully aware of the real cost of the depletion of natural capital. Information asymmetry is a third reason—often the link between cause and effect is obscured, making it difficult for actors to make informed choices. Cohen and Winn close with the realization that contrary to economic theory many firms are not perfect optimisers. They postulate that firms often do not optimise resource allocation because they are caught in a "business as usual" mentality.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Education">
       Education
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainable_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Education">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Higher education in sustainability across education streams including engineering, finance, supply chain and operations is gaining weight-age. Multiple institutes including Wharton, Columbia, CASI Global New York offer certifications in Sustainability. Corporate's prefer employees certified in sustainability. Reference
      <a class="external free" href="https://www.wharton.upenn.edu/" rel="nofollow">
       https://www.wharton.upenn.edu/
      </a>
      <a class="external free" href="http://www.columbia.edu/" rel="nofollow">
       http://www.columbia.edu/
      </a>
      <a class="external free" href="http://casiglobal.us/" rel="nofollow">
       http://casiglobal.us/
      </a>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">
       Criticism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainable_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Criticism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      It has been argued that since the 1960s, the concept of sustainable development has changed from 'conservation management' to 'economic development', whereby the original meaning of the concept has been stretched somewhat.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-to01_98-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-to01-98">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :48–54
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the 1960s, the international community realised that many African countries needed national plans to safeguard wildlife habitats, and that rural areas had to confront the limits imposed by soil, climate and water availability. This was a strategy of conservation management. In the 70s, however, the focus shifted to the broader issues of the provisioning of basic human needs, community participation as well as appropriate technology use throughout the developing countries (and not just in Africa). This was a strategy of economic development, and the strategy was carried even further by the Brundtland Report when the issues went from regional to international in scope and application.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-to01_98-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-to01-98">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :48–54
      </sup>
      In effect, the conservationists were crowded out and superseded by the developers.
     </p>
     <p>
      But shifting the focus of sustainable development from conservation to development has had the imperceptible effect of stretching the original forest management term of
      <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_yield" title="Sustainable yield">
       sustainable yield
      </a>
      from the use of renewable resources only (like forestry), to now also accounting for the use of non-renewable resources (like
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource#Classification" title="Natural resource">
       minerals
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kt01_99-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-kt01-99">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :13
      </sup>
      This stretching of the term has been questioned. Thus, environmental economist Kerry Turner has argued that literally, there can be no such thing as overall 'sustainable development' in an industrialised
      <a href="/wiki/World_economy" title="World economy">
       world economy
      </a>
      that remains heavily dependent on the extraction of Earth's finite stock of exhaustible mineral resources:
     </p>
     <table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
      <tr>
       <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">
        “
       </td>
       <td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">
        It makes no sense to talk about the sustainable use of a non-renewable resource (even with substantial
        <a href="/wiki/Recycling" title="Recycling">
         recycling
        </a>
        effort and use rates). Any positive rate of exploitation will eventually lead to exhaustion of the finite stock.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kt01_99-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-kt01-99">
          [99]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         :13
        </sup>
       </td>
       <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">
        ”
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      In effect, it has been argued that the
      <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">
       Industrial Revolution
      </a>
      as a whole is unsustainable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        [100]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :20f
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hd01_38-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-hd01-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :22f
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jr01_101-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-jr01-101">
        [101]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :61–67
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One critic has argued that the Brundtland Report promoted nothing but a business as usual strategy for world development, with the ambiguous and insubstantial concept of 'sustainable development' attached as a public relations slogan: The report was largely the result of a political bargaining process involving many special interest groups, all put together to create a common appeal of political acceptability across borders. After World War II, the notion of 'development' had been established in the West to imply the projection of the American model of society onto the rest of the world. In the 1970s and 1980s, this notion was broadened somewhat to also imply human rights, basic human needs and finally, ecological issues. The emphasis of the report was on helping poor nations out of poverty and meeting the basic needs of their growing populations — as usual. This issue demanded more economic growth, also in the rich countries, who would then import more goods from the poor countries to help them out — as usual. When the discussion switched to global ecological limits to growth, the obvious dilemma was left aside by calling for economic growth with improved resource efficiency, or what was termed 'a change in the
      <i>
       quality
      </i>
      of growth'. However, most countries in the West had experienced such improved resource efficiency since the early 20th century already and as usual; only, this improvement had been more than offset by continuing industrial expansion, to the effect that world resource consumption was now higher than ever before — and these two historical trends were completely ignored in the report. Taken together, the policy of perpetual economic growth for the entire planet remained virtually intact. Since the publication of the Brundtland Report, the ambiguous and insubstantial slogan of 'sustainable development' has marched on worldwide, the critic concludes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        [102]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :94-99
      </sup>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="External_links">
       External links
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainable_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: External links">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="https://www.arghand.org/" rel="nofollow">
        Arghand
       </a>
       , a collective that makes beauty products from sustainably harvested crops in Afghanistan.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.carpenterswithoutborders.org/" rel="nofollow">
        Carpenters Without Borders
       </a>
       , a non-profit that teaches reforestation and furniture building skills in developing countries.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www2.warwick.ac.uk/fac/cross_fac/gsd/" rel="nofollow">
        Global Sustainable Development
       </a>
       , an undergraduate degree program offered by the University of Warwick.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.circlesofsustainability.org/" rel="nofollow">
        Circles of Sustainability
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.greenwoodglobal.org/" rel="nofollow">
        Greenwood Global
       </a>
       , a non-profit that teaches furniture building skills and sustainable forestry practices in developing countries.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.webpages.uidaho.edu/sustainability/" rel="nofollow">
        Principles of Sustainability
       </a>
       , an open course offered by the University of Idaho and Washington State University.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external autonumber" href="http://casiglobal.us/" rel="nofollow">
        [3]
       </a>
       , CASI Global, New York is generally referred to as the global certification body. and has a presence across 58 countries (September 2016)
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.hksyu.edu/srerc/en/" rel="nofollow">
        Sustainable Real Estate Research Center, Hong Kong Shue Yan University
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.oreworld.org/" rel="nofollow">
        ORGANIZATION FOR THE REHABILITATION OF THE ENVIRONMENT
       </a>
       , encourages cultivation of bamboo and trees to rehabilitate deforestation.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://sustainabledevelopment.un.org/" rel="nofollow">
        Sustainable Development Knowledge Platform
       </a>
       , United Nations platform on sustainable development.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.wcl.american.edu/org/sustainabledevelopment" rel="nofollow">
        Sustainable Development Law &amp; Policy
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.sd-commission.org.uk/" rel="nofollow">
        UK Sustainable Development Commission
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://unsdsn.org" rel="nofollow">
        United Nations Sustainable Development Solutions Network
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.youtube.com/eugadproject" rel="nofollow">
        Vrinda Project Channel
       </a>
       with videos on MDGs connected to the Wikibook
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wikibooks-logo.svg" title="Wikibooks">
        <img alt="Wikibooks" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="300" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Wikibooks-logo.svg/14px-Wikibooks-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Wikibooks-logo.svg/21px-Wikibooks-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Wikibooks-logo.svg/28px-Wikibooks-logo.svg.png 2x" width="14"/>
       </a>
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Development_Cooperation_Handbook" title="b:Development Cooperation Handbook">
         Development Cooperation Handbook
        </a>
       </i>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://go.worldbank.org/7IS3KBWK20" rel="nofollow">
        World Bank
       </a>
       website on sustainable development.
      </li>
